<p><b>Wheat</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">grass</a> widely <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">cultivated</a> for its <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seed</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereal</a> <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grain</a> that is a worldwide staple food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shewry_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shewry-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mauseth2014_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mauseth2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belderok_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belderok-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_of_wheat" title="Taxonomy of wheat">many species of wheat</a> together make up the genus <i><b>Triticum</b></i>; the most widely grown is <a href="/wiki/Common_wheat" title="Common wheat">common wheat</a> (<i>T. aestivum</i>).  The <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_record" title="Archaeological record">archaeological record</a> suggests that wheat was first cultivated in the regions of the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a> around 9600 BCE. Botanically, the wheat kernel is a type of fruit called a <a href="/wiki/Caryopsis" title="Caryopsis">caryopsis</a>.
</p><p>Wheat is grown on more land area than any other food crop (220.4 million hectares or 545 million acres, 2014).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao-area2014_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-fao-area2014-5">[5]</a></sup> World trade in wheat is greater than for all other crops combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WheatBread_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-WheatBread-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2020, world production of wheat was 761 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> (839 million short tons; 1.7 trillion pounds), making it the second most-produced <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereal</a> after <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao-prod2014_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-fao-prod2014-7">[7]</a></sup> Since 1960, world production of wheat and other grain crops has tripled and is expected to grow further through the middle of the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-godfray_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-godfray-8">[8]</a></sup> Global demand for wheat is increasing due to the unique <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viscoelastic" title="Viscoelastic">viscoelastic</a> and adhesive properties of <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a> proteins, which facilitate the production of processed foods, whose consumption is increasing as a result of the worldwide industrialization process and the <a href="/wiki/Western_pattern_diet" title="Western pattern diet">westernization of the diet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry15_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-shewry15-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DayAugustin2006_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-DayAugustin2006-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wheat is an important source of <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry15_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-shewry15-9">[9]</a></sup> Globally, it is the leading source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetable_proteins" title="Vegetable proteins">vegetable proteins</a> in human food, having a protein content of about 13%, which is relatively high compared to other major cereals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CORDIS2016_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-CORDIS2016-11">[11]</a></sup> but relatively low in <a href="/wiki/Protein_quality" title="Protein quality">protein quality</a> for supplying <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOcerealsquality_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOcerealsquality-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOproteinquality_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOproteinquality-13">[13]</a></sup> When eaten as the <a href="/wiki/Whole_grain" title="Whole grain">whole grain</a>, wheat is a source of multiple <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry15_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-shewry15-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a small part of the general population, gluten – the major part of wheat protein – can trigger <a href="/wiki/Coeliac_disease" title="Coeliac disease">coeliac disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-celiac_gluten_sensitivity" title="Non-celiac gluten sensitivity">noncoeliac gluten sensitivity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gluten_ataxia" title="Gluten ataxia">gluten ataxia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis_herpetiformis" title="Dermatitis herpetiformis">dermatitis herpetiformis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LudvigssonLeffler_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-LudvigssonLeffler-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_history">Origin and history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origin and history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Usdaeinkorn1_Triticum_monococcum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Usdaeinkorn1_Triticum_monococcum.jpg/170px-Usdaeinkorn1_Triticum_monococcum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Usdaeinkorn1_Triticum_monococcum.jpg/255px-Usdaeinkorn1_Triticum_monococcum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Usdaeinkorn1_Triticum_monococcum.jpg/340px-Usdaeinkorn1_Triticum_monococcum.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Usdaeinkorn1_Triticum_monococcum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spikelets of a hulled wheat, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einkorn" title="Einkorn">einkorn</a></div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woman_harvesting_wheat,_Raisen_district,_Madhya_Pradesh,_India_ggia_version.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2472" data-file-width="3532" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Woman_harvesting_wheat%2C_Raisen_district%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India_ggia_version.jpg/220px-Woman_harvesting_wheat%2C_Raisen_district%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India_ggia_version.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Woman_harvesting_wheat%2C_Raisen_district%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India_ggia_version.jpg/330px-Woman_harvesting_wheat%2C_Raisen_district%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India_ggia_version.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Woman_harvesting_wheat%2C_Raisen_district%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India_ggia_version.jpg/440px-Woman_harvesting_wheat%2C_Raisen_district%2C_Madhya_Pradesh%2C_India_ggia_version.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Woman_harvesting_wheat,_Raisen_district,_Madhya_Pradesh,_India_ggia_version.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woman harvesting wheat, Raise district, Madhya Pradesh, India</div></div></div>
<p>Cultivation and repeated harvesting and sowing of the grains of wild grasses led to the creation of domestic strains, as mutant forms ('sports') of wheat were preferentially chosen by farmers. In domesticated wheat, grains are larger, and the seeds (inside the spikelets) remain attached to the ear by a toughened <a href="/wiki/Rachis" title="Rachis">rachis</a> during harvesting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In wild strains, a more fragile rachis allows the ear to easily <a href="/wiki/Shattering_(agriculture)" title="Shattering (agriculture)">shatter</a> and disperse the spikelets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Selection for larger grains and non-shattering heads by farmers might not have been deliberately intended, but simply have occurred because these traits made gathering the seeds easier; nevertheless such 'incidental' selection was an important part of crop <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a>. As the traits that improve wheat as a food source also involve the loss of the plant's natural seed dispersal mechanisms, highly domesticated strains of wheat cannot survive in the wild.
</p><p>Archaeological analysis of wild <a href="/wiki/Emmer" title="Emmer">emmer</a> indicates that it was first cultivated in the southern <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, with finds dating back as far as 9600 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColledgeArchaeology2007_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColledgeArchaeology2007-18">[18]</a></sup> Genetic analysis of wild <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einkorn" title="Einkorn">einkorn</a></i> wheat suggests that it was first grown in the <a href="/wiki/Karaca_Da%C4%9F" title="Karaca Dağ">Karacadaǧ Mountains</a> in southeastern Turkey. Dated archeological remains of einkorn wheat in settlement sites near this region, including those at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Hureyra" title="Abu Hureyra">Abu Hureyra</a> in Syria, suggest the domestication of einkorn near the Karacadag Mountain Range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> With the anomalous exception of two grains from <a href="/wiki/Iraq_ed-Dubb" title="Iraq ed-Dubb">Iraq ed-Dubb</a>, the earliest <a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14">carbon-14</a> date for einkorn wheat remains at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Hureyra" title="Abu Hureyra">Abu Hureyra</a> is 7800 to 7500 years BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Remains of harvested emmer from several sites near the Karacadag Range have been dated to between 8600 (at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cayonu" title="Cayonu">Cayonu</a>) and 8400 BC (Abu Hureyra), that is, in the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period. With the exception of Iraq ed-Dubb, the earliest carbon-14 dated remains of domesticated emmer wheat were found in the earliest levels of <a href="/wiki/Tell_Aswad" title="Tell Aswad">Tell Aswad</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> basin, near <a href="/wiki/Mount_Hermon" title="Mount Hermon">Mount Hermon</a> in Syria. These remains were dated by <a href="/wiki/Willem_van_Zeist" title="Willem van Zeist">Willem van Zeist</a> and his assistant Johanna Bakker-Heeres to 8800 BC. They also concluded that the settlers of Tell Aswad did not develop this form of emmer themselves, but brought the domesticated grains with them from an as yet unidentified location elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The cultivation of emmer reached Greece, Cyprus and <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> by 6500 BC, Egypt shortly after 6000 BC, and Germany and Spain by 5000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>  "The early Egyptians were developers of bread and the use of the oven and developed baking into one of the first large-scale food production industries."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> By 4000 BC, wheat had reached the British Isles and Scandinavia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SmithMomber2015_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-SmithMomber2015-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BraceDiekmann2019_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-BraceDiekmann2019-26">[26]</a></sup> Wheat likely appeared in China's lower Yellow River around 2600 Before Common Era (BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest evidence for hexaploid wheat has been confirmed through DNA analysis of wheat seeds, dating to around 6400–6200 BC, recovered from <a href="/wiki/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="Çatalhöyük">Çatalhöyük</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bilgic_2016_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bilgic_2016-28">[28]</a></sup> The first identifiable bread wheat (<i>Triticum aestivum</i>) with sufficient gluten for yeasted breads has been identified using DNA analysis in samples from a granary dating to approximately 1350 BC at <a href="/wiki/Assiros" title="Assiros">Assiros</a> in Macedonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>From Asia, wheat continued to spread across Europe and to the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a>. In the British Isles, wheat straw (thatch) was used for roofing in the Bronze Age, and was in common use until the late 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>White wheat bread was historically a high status food, but during the nineteenth century it became in Britain an item of mass consumption, displacing oats, barley and rye from diets in the North of the country.  It became "a sign of a high degree of culture".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>  After 1860, the enormous expansion of wheat production in the United States flooded the world market, lowering prices by 40%, and (along with the expansion of potato growing) made a major contribution to the nutritional welfare of the poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Farming_techniques">Farming techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Farming techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader"><b>Monocrop harvesting cycle</b></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_panorama.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1008" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Wheat_panorama.jpg/300px-Wheat_panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Wheat_panorama.jpg/450px-Wheat_panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Wheat_panorama.jpg/600px-Wheat_panorama.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Green wheat near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porterville,_Western_Cape" title="Porterville, Western Cape">Porterville</a> in South Africa. Wheat usually grown in large fields like this as a mono-crop</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_panorama_ripe.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="944" data-file-width="4064" decoding="async" height="70" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Wheat_panorama_ripe.jpg/300px-Wheat_panorama_ripe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Wheat_panorama_ripe.jpg/450px-Wheat_panorama_ripe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Wheat_panorama_ripe.jpg/600px-Wheat_panorama_ripe.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The same field later in the same year just before harvest. The wheat has turned a golden yellow colour indicating that it is ready for harvest.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Technological advances in soil preparation and seed placement at planting time, use of <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a> to improve plant growth, and advances in harvesting methods have all combined to promote wheat as a viable crop. When the use of <a href="/wiki/Seed_drill" title="Seed drill">seed drills</a> replaced broadcasting sowing of seed in the 18th century, another great increase in productivity occurred.
</p><p>Yields of pure wheat per unit area increased as methods of crop rotation were applied to long cultivated land, and the use of fertilizers became widespread. Improved agricultural husbandry has more recently included threshing machines, <a href="/wiki/Reaper-binder" title="Reaper-binder">reaper-binder</a> machines (the '<a href="/wiki/Combine_harvester" title="Combine harvester">combine harvester</a>'), <a href="/wiki/Tractor" title="Tractor">tractor</a>-drawn cultivators and planters, and better varieties (see <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norin_10_wheat" title="Norin 10 wheat">Norin 10 wheat</a>). Great expansion of wheat production occurred as new arable land was farmed in the Americas and Australia in the 19th and 20th centuries.
</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 251.33333333333px"><div style="width: 251.33333333333px">

</p>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Leaves emerge from the shoot apical <a href="/wiki/Meristem" title="Meristem">meristem</a> in a telescoping fashion until the transition to reproduction i.e. flowering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The last leaf produced by a wheat plant is known as the flag leaf. It is denser and has a higher <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> rate than other leaves, to supply <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a> to the developing ear. In temperate countries the flag leaf, along with the second and third highest leaf on the plant, supply the majority of carbohydrate in the grain and their condition is paramount to yield formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Wheat is unusual among plants in having more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomata" title="Stomata">stomata</a> on the upper (adaxial) side of the leaf, than on the under (abaxial) side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> It has been theorised that this might be an effect of it having been <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a> and cultivated longer than any other plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Winter_wheat" title="Winter wheat">Winter wheat</a> generally produces up to 15 leaves per shoot and spring wheat up to 9<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheat_Growth_Guide_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheat_Growth_Guide-39">[39]</a></sup> and winter crops may have up to 35 tillers (shoots) per plant (depending on cultivar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheat_Growth_Guide_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wheat_Growth_Guide-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wheat <a href="/wiki/Root" title="Root">roots</a> are among the deepest of arable crops, extending as far down as 2 metres (6 ft 7 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> While the roots of a wheat plant are growing, the plant also accumulates an energy store in its stem, in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fructans" title="Fructans">fructans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> which helps the plant to yield under drought and disease pressure,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> but it has been observed that there is a trade-off between root growth and stem non-structural carbohydrate reserves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Root growth is likely to be prioritised in drought-adapted crops, while stem non-structural carbohydrate is prioritised in varieties developed for countries where disease is a bigger issue.
</p><p>Depending on variety, wheat may be <a href="/wiki/Awn_(botany)" title="Awn (botany)">awned</a> or not awned. Producing awns incurs a cost in grain number,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> but wheat awns photosynthesise more efficiently than their leaves with regards to water usage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> so awns are much more frequent in varieties of wheat grown in hot drought-prone countries than those generally seen in temperate countries. For this reason, awned varieties could become more widely grown due to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>. In Europe, however, a decline in <a href="/wiki/Climate_resilience" title="Climate resilience">climate resilience</a> of wheat has been observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Genetics"></span><span class="anchor" id="Breeding"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics_and_breeding">Genetics and breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Genetics and breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In traditional agricultural systems wheat populations often consist of <a href="/wiki/Landrace" title="Landrace">landraces</a>, informal farmer-maintained populations that often maintain high levels of morphological diversity. Although landraces of wheat are no longer grown in Europe and North America, they continue to be important elsewhere. The origins of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crop_breeding" title="Crop breeding">formal wheat breeding</a> lie in the nineteenth century, when single line varieties were created through selection of seed from a single plant noted to have desired properties. Modern wheat breeding developed in the first years of the twentieth century and was closely linked to the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mendelian_genetics" title="Mendelian genetics">Mendelian genetics</a>. The standard method of breeding inbred wheat cultivars is by crossing two lines using hand emasculation, then selfing or inbreeding the progeny. Selections are <i>identified</i> (shown to have the genes responsible for the varietal differences) ten or more generations before release as a variety or cultivar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bajaj_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bajaj-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Major breeding objectives include high grain yield, good quality, disease and insect resistance and tolerance to abiotic stresses, including mineral, moisture and heat tolerance. The major diseases in temperate environments include the following, arranged in a rough order of their significance from cooler to warmer climates: <a href="/wiki/Eyespot_(wheat)" title="Eyespot (wheat)">eyespot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phaeosphaeria_nodorum" title="Phaeosphaeria nodorum">Stagonospora nodorum blotch</a> (also known as glume blotch), <a href="/wiki/Wheat_yellow_rust" title="Wheat yellow rust">yellow</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wheat_yellow_rust" title="Wheat yellow rust">stripe rust</a>, <a href="/wiki/Powdery_mildew#Powdery_mildew_of_wheat" title="Powdery mildew">powdery mildew</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Septoria_tritici" title="Septoria tritici">Septoria tritici</a> blotch (sometimes known as leaf blotch), <a href="/wiki/Wheat_leaf_rust" title="Wheat leaf rust">brown</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wheat_leaf_rust" title="Wheat leaf rust">leaf rust</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheat_fusarium_head_blight" title="Wheat fusarium head blight">Fusarium head blight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyrenophora_tritici-repentis" title="Pyrenophora tritici-repentis">tan spot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stem_rust" title="Stem rust">stem rust</a>. In tropical areas, <a href="/wiki/Spot_blotch_(wheat)" title="Spot blotch (wheat)">spot blotch</a> (also known as Helminthosporium leaf blight) is also important.
</p><p>Wheat has also been the subject of <a href="/wiki/Mutation_breeding" title="Mutation breeding">mutation breeding</a>, with the use of gamma, x-rays, ultraviolet light, and sometimes harsh chemicals. The varieties of wheat created through these methods are in the hundreds (going as far back as 1960), more of them being created in higher populated countries such as China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Bread wheat with high grain iron and zinc content has been developed through gamma radiation breeding,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> and through conventional selection breeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIMMYT-Ravi-Singh-PBS_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIMMYT-Ravi-Singh-PBS-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>International wheat breeding is led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIMMYT" title="CIMMYT">CIMMYT</a> in Mexico. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICARDA" title="ICARDA">ICARDA</a> is another major public sector international wheat breeder, but it was forced to relocate from Syria in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" title="Syrian Civil War">Syrian Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICARDA-conflict_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICARDA-conflict-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yields">Yields</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Yields">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The presence of certain versions of wheat genes has been important for crop yields. Genes for the 'dwarfing' trait, first used by <a href="/wiki/Norin_10_wheat" title="Norin 10 wheat">Japanese wheat breeders</a> to produce short-stalked wheat, have had a huge effect on wheat yields worldwide, and were major factors in the success of the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> in Mexico and Asia, an initiative led by <a href="/wiki/Norman_Borlaug" title="Norman Borlaug">Norman Borlaug</a>. Dwarfing genes enable the carbon that is fixed in the plant during photosynthesis to be diverted towards seed production, and they also help prevent the problem of lodging. "Lodging" occurs when an ear stalk falls over in the wind and rots on the ground, and heavy nitrogenous fertilization of wheat makes the grass grow taller and become more susceptible to this problem. By 1997, 81% of the developing world's wheat area was planted to semi-dwarf wheats, giving both increased yields and better response to nitrogenous fertilizer.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triticum_turgidum_subsp._polonicum" title="Triticum turgidum subsp. polonicum"><i>T. turgidum</i> subsp. <i>polonicum</i></a> is known for its longer <a href="/wiki/Glume" title="Glume">glumes</a> and grains, has been bred into main wheat lines for its grain size effect, and likely has contributed these traits to <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Triticum_petropavlovskyi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Triticum petropavlovskyi (page does not exist)">T. petropavlovskyi</a></i> and the Portuguese <a href="/wiki/Landrace" title="Landrace">landrace</a> group "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Arrancada&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Arrancada (page does not exist)">Arrancada</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adamski-et-al-2021_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adamski-et-al-2021-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen-2021_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chen-2021-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>As with many plants, <a href="/wiki/MADS-box" title="MADS-box">MADS-box</a> influences flower development, and more specifically, as with other agricultural Poaceae, heavily influences the total weight output at the end of the entire grain growing process. Despite that importance, as of 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> little research has been done into MADS-box and other such spikelet and flower genetics in wheat specifically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adamski-et-al-2021_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adamski-et-al-2021-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The world record wheat yield is about 17 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> per <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectare</a> (15,000 pounds per acre), reached in New Zealand in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> A project in the UK, led by <a href="/wiki/Rothamsted_Research" title="Rothamsted Research">Rothamsted Research</a> has aimed to raise wheat yields in the country to 20 t/ha (18,000 lb/acre) by 2020, but in 2018 the UK record stood at 16 t/ha (14,000 lb/acre), and the average yield was just 8 t/ha (7,100 lb/acre).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disease_resistance">Disease resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Disease resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wild grasses in the genus <i>Triticum</i> and related genera, and grasses such as <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a> have been a source of many disease-resistance traits for cultivated wheat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transgenic_plant" title="Transgenic plant">breeding</a> since the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Plant_disease_resistance" title="Plant disease resistance">resistance genes</a> have been identified against <i><a href="/wiki/Pyrenophora_tritici-repentis" title="Pyrenophora tritici-repentis">Pyrenophora tritici-repentis</a></i>, especially races 1 and 5, those most problematic in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHEAT-Kazakhstan-GWAS_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHEAT-Kazakhstan-GWAS-58">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crop_wild_relative" title="Crop wild relative">Wild relative</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Aegilops_tauschii" title="Aegilops tauschii">Aegilops tauschii</a></i> is the source of several genes effective against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TTKSK" title="TTKSK">TTKSK</a>/Ug99 - <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sr33_(gene)" title="Sr33 (gene)">Sr33</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sr45&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sr45 (page does not exist)">Sr45</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sr46&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sr46 (page does not exist)">Sr46</a></i>, and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SrTA1662&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SrTA1662 (page does not exist)">SrTA1662</a></i> - of which <i>Sr33</i> and <i>SrTA1662</i> are the work of Olson et al. 2013, and <i>Sr45</i> and <i>Sr46</i> are also briefly reviewed therein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bohra-et-al-2021_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bohra-et-al-2021-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023754711">.mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#b1d2ff}</style><span class="vanchor"><span id="Lr67"></span><span class="vanchor-text">Lr67</span></span></i> is an <a href="/wiki/R_gene" title="R gene">R gene</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominant_negative" title="Dominant negative">dominant negative</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partial_adult_plant_resistance" title="Partial adult plant resistance">partial adult resistance</a> discovered and molecularly characterized by Moore et al. 2015. As of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> <i>Lr67</i> is effective against all races of <a href="/wiki/Wheat_leaf_rust" title="Wheat leaf rust">leaf</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheat_stripe_rust" title="Wheat stripe rust">stripe</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheat_stem_rust" title="Wheat stem rust">stem</a> rusts, and  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheat_powdery_mildew" title="Wheat powdery mildew">powdery mildew</a> (<i>Blumeria graminis</i>). This is produced by a <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutation</a> of two <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">aminos</a> in what is <a href="/wiki/Gene_prediction" title="Gene prediction">predicted to be</a> a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexose_transporter" title="Hexose transporter">hexose transporter</a>. The product then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterodimerization" title="Heterodimerization">heterodimerizes</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_susceptibility_allele" title="Plant susceptibility allele">susceptible's</a> product, with the downstream result of reducing <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> uptake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kourelis-Hoorn-2018_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kourelis-Hoorn-2018-60">[60]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1023754711" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="vanchor"><span id="Lr34"></span><span class="vanchor-text">Lr34</span></span></i> is widely deployed in cultivars due to its abnormally broad effectiveness, conferring resistance against <a href="/wiki/Wheat_leaf_rust" title="Wheat leaf rust">leaf-</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheat_stripe_rust" title="Wheat stripe rust">stripe-rusts</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheat_powdery_mildew" title="Wheat powdery mildew">powdery mildew</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodds-Rathjen-2010_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dodds-Rathjen-2010-61">[61]</a></sup> Krattinger et al. 2009 finds <i>Lr34</i> to also be an <a href="/wiki/ATP-binding_cassette_transporter" title="ATP-binding cassette transporter">ABC transporter</a> and conclude that this is the probably means of its effectiveness<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodds-Rathjen-2010_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dodds-Rathjen-2010-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furbank-Tester-2011_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank-Tester-2011-62">[62]</a></sup> and the reason that it produces a 'slow rusting'/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adult_plant_resistance" title="Adult plant resistance">adult resistance</a> phenotype.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furbank-Tester-2011_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank-Tester-2011-62">[62]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1023754711" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="vanchor"><span id="Fusarium_head_blight_resistance"></span><span class="vanchor-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusarium_head_blight_resistance" title="Fusarium head blight resistance">Resistance to Fusarium head blight</a></span></span> (FHB, Fusarium ear blight) is also an important breeding target. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marker-assisted_breeding" title="Marker-assisted breeding">Marker-assisted breeding</a> panels involving <a href="/wiki/Kompetitive_allele_specific_PCR" title="Kompetitive allele specific PCR">kompetitive allele specific PCR</a> can be used. Singh et al. 2019 identify a KASP <a href="/wiki/Genetic_marker" title="Genetic marker">genetic marker</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Pore-forming_toxin" title="Pore-forming toxin">pore-forming toxin</a>-like gene providing FHB resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaur-et-al-2020_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaur-et-al-2020-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid_wheats">Hybrid wheats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hybrid wheats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because wheat self-pollinates, creating <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_seed" title="Hybrid seed">hybrid seed</a> is extremely labor-intensive; the high cost of hybrid wheat seed relative to its moderate benefits have kept farmers from adopting them widely<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> despite nearly 90 years of effort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/F1_hybrid" title="F1 hybrid">F1 hybrid</a> wheat cultivars should not be confused with wheat cultivars deriving from standard <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">plant breeding</a>, which may descend from hybrid crosses further back in its ancestry. <a href="/wiki/Heterosis" title="Heterosis">Heterosis</a> or hybrid vigor (as in the familiar F1 hybrids of maize) occurs in common (hexaploid) wheat, but it is difficult to produce seed of hybrid cultivars on a commercial scale as is done with maize because wheat flowers are perfect in the botanical sense, meaning they have both male and female parts, and normally <a href="/wiki/Self-pollination" title="Self-pollination">self-pollinate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bajaj_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bajaj-47">[47]</a></sup> Commercial hybrid wheat seed has been produced using chemical hybridizing agents, <a href="/wiki/Plant_hormone" title="Plant hormone">plant growth regulators</a> that selectively interfere with pollen development, or naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasmic_male_sterility" title="Cytoplasmic male sterility">cytoplasmic male sterility</a> systems. Hybrid wheat has been a limited commercial success in Europe (particularly France), the United States and South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Synthetic hexaploids made by crossing the wild goatgrass wheat ancestor <i><a href="/wiki/Aegilops_tauschii" title="Aegilops tauschii">Aegilops tauschii</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and various other <i><a href="/wiki/Aegilops" title="Aegilops">Aegilops</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kishii-2019_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kishii-2019-69">[69]</a></sup> and various durum wheats are now being deployed, and these increase the genetic diversity of cultivated wheats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Triticale:_Wheat-rye_hybrid">Triticale: Wheat-rye hybrid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Triticale: Wheat-rye hybrid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheat,_rye,_triticale_montage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="253" data-file-width="564" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Wheat%2C_rye%2C_triticale_montage.jpg/280px-Wheat%2C_rye%2C_triticale_montage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Wheat%2C_rye%2C_triticale_montage.jpg/420px-Wheat%2C_rye%2C_triticale_montage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Wheat%2C_rye%2C_triticale_montage.jpg/560px-Wheat%2C_rye%2C_triticale_montage.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wheat,_rye,_triticale_montage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The smaller grain of wheat on the left, larger kernels of <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a> next, and triticale on the right – triticale grain is significantly larger than wheat.</div></div></div>
<p>In ancient times, wheat was often considered a luxury grain because it had lower yield but better taste and digestibility than competitors like rye. In the 19th century, efforts were made to hybridize the two to get a crop with the best traits of both. This produced <a href="/wiki/Triticale" title="Triticale">triticale</a>, a grain with high potential, but fraught with problems relating to fertility and germination. These have mostly been solved, so that in the 20th century millions of acres of triticale are being grown worldwide.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gluten">Gluten</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gluten">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modern bread wheat varieties have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breeding_(plant)" title="Breeding (plant)">cross-bred</a> to contain greater amounts of gluten,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> which affords significant advantages for improving the quality of breads and pastas from a functional point of view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-delcour_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-delcour-74">[74]</a></sup> However, a 2020 study that grew and analyzed 60 wheat cultivars from between 1891 and 2010 found no changes in albumin/globulin and gluten contents over time. "Overall, the harvest year had a more significant effect on protein composition than the cultivar. At the protein level, we found no evidence to support an increased <a href="/wiki/Immunostimulant" title="Immunostimulant">immunostimulatory</a> potential of modern winter wheat."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scherf_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scherf-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_efficiency">Water efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Water efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stoma" title="Stoma">Stomata</a> (or leaf pores) are involved in both uptake of carbon dioxide gas from the atmosphere and water vapor losses from the leaf due to water <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a>. Basic physiological investigation of these gas exchange processes has yielded valuable carbon <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> based methods that are used for breeding wheat varieties with improved water-use efficiency. These varieties can improve crop productivity in rain-fed dry-land wheat farms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insect_resistance">Insect resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Insect resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The gene <i>Sm1</i> protects against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orange_wheat_blossom_midge" title="Orange wheat blossom midge">orange wheat blossom midge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kassa-et-al-2016_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kassa-et-al-2016-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USask-15-varieties-Dhaka-Tribune_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-USask-15-varieties-Dhaka-Tribune-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USask-15-varieties_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-USask-15-varieties-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walkowiak-et-al-2020_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walkowiak-et-al-2020-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genome">Genome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Genome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2010, a team of UK scientists funded by <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology_and_Biological_Sciences_Research_Council" title="Biotechnology and Biological Sciences Research Council">BBSRC</a> announced they had decoded the wheat genome for the first time (95% of the genome of a variety of wheat known as Chinese Spring line 42).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> This genome was released in a basic format for scientists and plant breeders to use but was not a fully annotated sequence which was reported in some of the media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> On 29 November 2012, an essentially complete gene set of bread wheat was published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature.com_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shotgun_sequencing" title="Shotgun sequencing">Random shotgun libraries</a> of total DNA and cDNA from the <i>T. aestivum</i> cv. Chinese Spring (CS42) were sequenced in Roche 454 pyrosequencer using GS FLX Titanium and GS FLX+ platforms to generate 85 Gb of sequence (220 million reads) and identified between 94,000 and 96,000 genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature.com_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-83">[83]</a></sup> The implications of the research in cereal genetics and breeding includes the examination of genome variation, analysis of population genetics and evolutionary biology, and further studying epigenetic modifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-currentscience.ac.in_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-currentscience.ac.in-84">[84]</a></sup> In 2018 an even more complete <i>Chinese Spring</i> genome was released by a different team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USask-CS-2018_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-USask-CS-2018-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Then in 2020 some of the same researchers produced 15 genome sequences from various locations and varieties around the world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USask-15-varieties-Dhaka-Tribune_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-USask-15-varieties-Dhaka-Tribune-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USask-15-varieties_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-USask-15-varieties-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walkowiak-et-al-2020_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walkowiak-et-al-2020-80">[80]</a></sup> – the most complete and detailed so far<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USask-15-varieties-Dhaka-Tribune_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-USask-15-varieties-Dhaka-Tribune-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USask-15-varieties_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-USask-15-varieties-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walkowiak-et-al-2020_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-Walkowiak-et-al-2020-80">[80]</a></sup> – along with examples of their own use of the sequences to localize particular insect and disease resistance factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USask-15-varieties-Dhaka-Tribune_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-USask-15-varieties-Dhaka-Tribune-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USask-15-varieties_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-USask-15-varieties-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walkowiak-et-al-2020_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-Walkowiak-et-al-2020-80">[80]</a></sup> The team expects these sequences will be useful in future cultivar breeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USask-15-varieties-Dhaka-Tribune_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-USask-15-varieties-Dhaka-Tribune-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USask-15-varieties_79-4"><a href="#cite_note-USask-15-varieties-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walkowiak-et-al-2020_80-4"><a href="#cite_note-Walkowiak-et-al-2020-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_engineering">Genetic engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Genetic engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="CRISPR.2FCas9"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="CRISPR/Cas9">CRISPR/Cas9</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: CRISPR/Cas9">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>For decades the primary <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_wheat" title="Genetically modified wheat">genetic modification</a> technique has been <a href="/wiki/Non-homologous_end_joining" title="Non-homologous end joining">non-homologous end joining (NHEJ)</a>. However, since its introduction, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CRISPR/Cas9" title="CRISPR/Cas9"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1023754711" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="vanchor"><span id="CRISPR"></span><span class="vanchor-text">CRISPR</span></span>/<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1023754711" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="vanchor"><span id="Cas9"></span><span class="vanchor-text">Cas9</span></span></a> tool has been extensively adopted, for example:
</p>
<ul><li>To intentionally damage three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homolog" title="Homolog">homologs</a> of  <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=TaNP1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="TaNP1 (page does not exist)">TaNP1</a></i> (a <a href="/wiki/Glucose-methanol-choline_oxidoreductase_family" title="Glucose-methanol-choline oxidoreductase family">glucose-methanol-choline oxidoreductase</a> gene) to produce a novel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Male_sterility" title="Male sterility">male sterility</a> trait, by Li et al. 2020<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li-et-al-2021_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li-et-al-2021-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blumeria_graminis_f.sp._tritici_resistance" title="Blumeria graminis f.sp. tritici resistance"><i>Blumeria graminis</i> f.sp. <i>tritici</i> resistance</a> has been produced by Shan et al. 2013 and Wang et al. 2014 by editing one of the <a href="/wiki/Mildew_resistance_locus_o" title="Mildew resistance locus o">mildew resistance locus o</a> genes (more specifically one of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TaMLO" title="TaMLO">Triticum aestivum MLO (TaMLO)</a></i> genes)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li-et-al-2021_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Li-et-al-2021-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=TaEDR1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="TaEDR1 (page does not exist)">Triticum aestivum EDR1 (TaEDR1)</a></i> (the <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=EDR1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="EDR1 (page does not exist)">EDR1</a></i> gene, which inhibits <i>Bmt</i> resistance) has been <a href="/wiki/Gene_knockout" title="Gene knockout">knocked out</a> by Zhang et al. 2017 to improve that resistance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li-et-al-2021_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-Li-et-al-2021-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=TaHRC&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="TaHRC (page does not exist)">Triticum aestivum HRC (TaHRC)</a></i> has been disabled by Su et al. 2019 thus producing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gibberella_zeae_resistance" title="Gibberella zeae resistance"><i>Gibberella zeae</i> resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li-et-al-2021_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-Li-et-al-2021-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=TaMs1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="TaMs1 (page does not exist)">Triticum aestivum Ms1 (TaMs1)</a></i> has been knocked out by Okada et al. 2019 to produce another novel male sterility<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li-et-al-2021_86-4"><a href="#cite_note-Li-et-al-2021-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li>and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TaALS" title="TaALS">Triticum aestivum acetolactate synthase (TaALS)</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TaACC" title="TaACC">Triticum aestivum acetyl-CoA-carboxylase (TaACC)</a></i> were subjected to base changes by Zhang et al. 2019 (in two publications) to confer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbicide_resistance" title="Herbicide resistance">herbicide resistance</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ALS_inhibitor" title="ALS inhibitor">ALS inhibitors</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ACCase_inhibitor" title="ACCase inhibitor">ACCase inhibitors</a> respectively<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li-et-al-2021_86-5"><a href="#cite_note-Li-et-al-2021-86">[86]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>As of 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> these examples illustrate the rapid deployment and results that CRISPR/Cas9 has shown in wheat disease resistance improvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li-et-al-2021_86-6"><a href="#cite_note-Li-et-al-2021-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are around 20 wheat varieties of 7 species grown throughout the world. In Canada different varieties are blended prior to sale. "Identity preserved" wheat that has been stored and transported separately (at extra cost) usually fetches a higher price.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from mutant versions of genes selected in antiquity during domestication, there has been more recent deliberate selection of <a href="/wiki/Allele" title="Allele">alleles</a> that affect growth characteristics. Some wheat species are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a>, with two sets of <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a>, but many are stable <a href="/wiki/Polyploidy" title="Polyploidy">polyploids</a>, with four sets of chromosomes (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetraploid" title="Tetraploid">tetraploid</a>) or six (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexaploid" title="Hexaploid">hexaploid</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hancock_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hancock-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einkorn" title="Einkorn">Einkorn</a> wheat (<i>T. monococcum</i>) is diploid (AA, two complements of seven chromosomes, 2n=14).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belderok_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belderok-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most tetraploid wheats (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Emmer" title="Emmer">emmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Durum" title="Durum">durum</a> wheat) are derived from <a href="/wiki/Emmer#Wild_emmer" title="Emmer">wild emmer</a>, <i>T. dicoccoides</i>. Wild emmer is itself the result of a hybridization between two diploid wild grasses, <i><a href="/wiki/Triticum_urartu" title="Triticum urartu">T. urartu</a></i> and a wild goatgrass such as <i>Aegilops searsii</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Aegilops_speltoides" title="Aegilops speltoides">Ae. speltoides</a></i>. The unknown grass has never been identified among non-extinct wild grasses, but the closest living relative is <i>Aegilops speltoides</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The hybridization that formed wild emmer (AABB) occurred in the wild, long before domestication,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hancock_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hancock-88">[88]</a></sup> and was driven by natural selection.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_harvest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="417" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Wheat_harvest.jpg/220px-Wheat_harvest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Wheat_harvest.jpg/330px-Wheat_harvest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Wheat_harvest.jpg/440px-Wheat_harvest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_harvest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wheat harvest on the <a href="/wiki/Palouse" title="Palouse">Palouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, United States</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WheatPennsylvania1943.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1525" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/WheatPennsylvania1943.jpg/220px-WheatPennsylvania1943.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/WheatPennsylvania1943.jpg/330px-WheatPennsylvania1943.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/WheatPennsylvania1943.jpg/440px-WheatPennsylvania1943.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WheatPennsylvania1943.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sheaved and <a href="/wiki/Stook" title="Stook">stooked</a> wheat</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eilensen_Mb_(2018).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1440" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Eilensen_Mb_%282018%29.jpg/220px-Eilensen_Mb_%282018%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Eilensen_Mb_%282018%29.jpg/330px-Eilensen_Mb_%282018%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Eilensen_Mb_%282018%29.jpg/440px-Eilensen_Mb_%282018%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eilensen_Mb_(2018).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traditional wheat sheafing machine</div></div></div>
<p>Hexaploid wheats evolved in farmers' fields. Either domesticated emmer or durum wheat hybridized with yet another wild diploid grass (<i><a href="/wiki/Aegilops_tauschii" title="Aegilops tauschii">Aegilops tauschii</a></i>) to make the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexaploid" title="Hexaploid">hexaploid</a> wheats, <a href="/wiki/Spelt" title="Spelt">spelt</a> wheat and <a href="/wiki/Common_wheat" title="Common wheat">bread wheat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hancock_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hancock-88">[88]</a></sup> These have <i>three</i> sets of paired chromosomes, three times as many as in diploid wheat.
</p><p>At the point of the end user – the farmer who is sowing and reaping – the exact variety they have in their field is usually not known. The development of genetic assays which can distinguish the small differences between cultivars is allowing that question to be answered field-by-field, for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BGRI-varieties_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-BGRI-varieties-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_cultivated_species_of_wheat">Major cultivated species of wheat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Major cultivated species of wheat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2016</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Hexaploid species
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Common_wheat" title="Common wheat">Common wheat</a></b> or <b>bread wheat</b> (<i>T. aestivum</i>) – A <a href="/wiki/Ploidy" title="Ploidy">hexaploid</a> species that is the most widely cultivated in the world.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Spelt" title="Spelt">Spelt</a></b> (<i>T. spelta</i>) – Another hexaploid species cultivated in limited quantities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (May 2017)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> Spelt is sometimes considered a subspecies<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (May 2017)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> of the closely related species <a href="/wiki/Common_wheat" title="Common wheat">common wheat</a> (<i>T. aestivum</i>), in which case its botanical name is considered to be <i>T. aestivum</i> ssp. <i>spelta</i>.</li></ul>
<p>Tetraploid species
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Durum" title="Durum">Durum</a></b> (<i>T. durum</i>) – A tetraploid form of wheat widely used today, and the second most widely cultivated wheat.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Emmer" title="Emmer">Emmer</a></b> (<i>T. dicoccum</i>) – A <a href="/wiki/Ploidy" title="Ploidy">tetraploid</a> species, cultivated in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">ancient times</a> but no longer in widespread use.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Khorasan_wheat" title="Khorasan wheat">Khorasan</a></b> (<i>T. turgidum ssp. turanicum</i>, also called <i>T. turanicum</i>) is a tetraploid wheat species. It is an ancient grain type; Khorasan refers to a historical region in modern-day Afghanistan and the northeast of Iran. This grain is twice the size of modern-day wheat and is known for its rich nutty flavor.</li></ul>
<p>Diploid species
</p>
<ul><li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einkorn" title="Einkorn">Einkorn</a></b> (<i>T. monococcum</i>) – A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a> species with wild and cultivated variants. Domesticated at the same time as emmer wheat.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hulled_versus_free-threshing_species">Hulled versus free-threshing species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hulled versus free-threshing species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Naked_and_hulled_wheat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="686" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Naked_and_hulled_wheat.jpg/220px-Naked_and_hulled_wheat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Naked_and_hulled_wheat.jpg/330px-Naked_and_hulled_wheat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Naked_and_hulled_wheat.jpg/440px-Naked_and_hulled_wheat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Naked_and_hulled_wheat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Left: Naked wheat, Bread wheat <i>Triticum aestivum</i>; Right: Hulled wheat, Einkorn, <i>Triticum monococcum</i>. Note how the einkorn ear breaks down into intact spikelets.</div></div></div><p> The four wild species of wheat, along with the domesticated varieties <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einkorn" title="Einkorn">einkorn</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Potts_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Potts-91">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Emmer" title="Emmer">emmer</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Spelt" title="Spelt">spelt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> have hulls. This more primitive morphology (in evolutionary terms) consists of toughened glumes that tightly enclose the grains, and (in domesticated wheats) a semi-brittle rachis that breaks easily on threshing.
</p><p>The result is that when threshed, the wheat ear breaks up into spikelets. To obtain the grain, further processing, such as milling or pounding, is needed to remove the hulls or husks. Hulled wheats are often stored as spikelets because the toughened glumes give good protection against pests of stored grain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Potts_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Potts-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>In free-threshing (or naked) forms, such as durum wheat and common wheat, the glumes are fragile and the rachis tough. On threshing, the chaff breaks up, releasing the grains.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">Naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_of_wheat" title="Taxonomy of wheat">Taxonomy of wheat</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_in_sack.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Wheat_in_sack.jpg/220px-Wheat_in_sack.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Wheat_in_sack.jpg/330px-Wheat_in_sack.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Wheat_in_sack.jpg/440px-Wheat_in_sack.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_in_sack.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sack of wheat <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grains</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modell_eines_Korns_von_Triticum_vulgare_(Weizen)_-Osterloh_Nr._138-.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Modell_eines_Korns_von_Triticum_vulgare_%28Weizen%29_-Osterloh_Nr._138-.jpg/220px-Modell_eines_Korns_von_Triticum_vulgare_%28Weizen%29_-Osterloh_Nr._138-.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Modell_eines_Korns_von_Triticum_vulgare_%28Weizen%29_-Osterloh_Nr._138-.jpg/330px-Modell_eines_Korns_von_Triticum_vulgare_%28Weizen%29_-Osterloh_Nr._138-.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Modell_eines_Korns_von_Triticum_vulgare_%28Weizen%29_-Osterloh_Nr._138-.jpg/440px-Modell_eines_Korns_von_Triticum_vulgare_%28Weizen%29_-Osterloh_Nr._138-.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Modell_eines_Korns_von_Triticum_vulgare_(Weizen)_-Osterloh_Nr._138-.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Model of a wheat grain, <a href="/wiki/Botanical_Museum_Greifswald" title="Botanical Museum Greifswald">Botanical Museum Greifswald</a></div></div></div>
<p>There are many botanical classification systems used for wheat species, discussed in a separate article on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheat_taxonomy" title="Wheat taxonomy">wheat taxonomy</a>. The name of a wheat species from one information source may not be the name of a wheat species in another.
</p><p>Within a species, wheat cultivars are further classified by wheat breeders and farmers in terms of:
</p>
<ul><li>Growing season, such as <a href="/wiki/Winter_wheat" title="Winter wheat">winter wheat</a> vs. spring wheat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CVDE_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-CVDE-94">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">Protein</a> content. Bread wheat protein content ranges from 10% in some soft wheats with high starch contents, to 15% in hard wheats.</li>
<li>The quality of the wheat protein <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>. This protein can determine the suitability of a wheat to a particular dish. A strong and elastic gluten present in bread wheats enables <a href="/wiki/Dough" title="Dough">dough</a> to trap carbon dioxide during leavening, but elastic gluten interferes with the rolling of pasta into thin sheets. The gluten protein in durum wheats used for pasta is strong but not elastic.</li>
<li>Grain color (red, white or amber). Many wheat varieties are reddish-brown due to phenolic compounds present in the bran layer which are transformed to pigments by browning enzymes. White wheats have a lower content of phenolics and browning enzymes, and are generally less astringent in taste than red wheats. The yellowish color of durum wheat and <a href="/wiki/Semolina" title="Semolina">semolina</a> flour made from it is due to a <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoid</a> pigment called <a href="/wiki/Lutein" title="Lutein">lutein</a>, which can be oxidized to a colorless form by enzymes present in the grain.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classes_used_in_North_America">Classes used in North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Classes used in North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The named classes of wheat in English are more or less the same in Canada as in the US, as broadly the same commercial cash crop strains can be found in both.
</p><p>The classes used in the <a href="/wiki/Wheat_production_in_the_United_States" title="Wheat production in the United States">United States</a> are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Durum" title="Durum">Durum</a></b> – Very hard, translucent, light-colored grain used to make <a href="/wiki/Semolina" title="Semolina">semolina</a> flour for pasta and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bulghur" title="Bulghur">bulghur</a>; high in protein, specifically, gluten protein.</li>
<li><b>Hard Red Spring</b> – Hard, brownish, high-<a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> wheat used for bread and hard baked goods. Bread flour and high-gluten flours are commonly made from hard red spring wheat. It is primarily traded on the <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis_Grain_Exchange" title="Minneapolis Grain Exchange">Minneapolis Grain Exchange</a>.</li>
<li><b>Hard Red Winter</b> – Hard, brownish, mellow high-protein wheat used for bread, hard baked goods and as an adjunct in other flours to increase protein in pastry flour for pie crusts. Some brands of unbleached all-purpose flours are commonly made from hard red winter wheat alone. It is primarily traded on the <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Board_of_Trade" title="Kansas City Board of Trade">Kansas City Board of Trade</a>. Many varieties grown from Kansas south are descendant from a variety known as "turkey red", which was brought to Kansas by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mennonite" title="Mennonite">Mennonite</a> immigrants from Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marquis_wheat" title="Marquis wheat">Marquis wheat</a> was developed to prosper in the shorter growing season in Canada, and is grown as far south as southern Nebraska.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Soft Red Winter</b> – Soft, low-protein wheat used for cakes, pie crusts, biscuits, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muffins" title="Muffins">muffins</a>. Cake flour, pastry flour, and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-rising_flour" title="Self-rising flour">self-rising flours</a> with <a href="/wiki/Baking_powder" title="Baking powder">baking powder</a> and salt added, for example, are made from soft red winter wheat. It is primarily traded on the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Board_of_Trade" title="Chicago Board of Trade">Chicago Board of Trade</a>.</li>
<li><b>Hard White</b> – Hard, light-colored, opaque, chalky, medium-protein wheat planted in dry, temperate areas. Used for bread and brewing.</li>
<li><b>Soft White</b> – Soft, light-colored, very low protein wheat grown in temperate moist areas. Used for pie crusts and pastry. Pastry flour, for example, is sometimes made from soft white winter wheat.</li></ul>
<p>Red wheats may need bleaching; therefore, white wheats usually command higher prices than red wheats on the commodities market.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_food">As a food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: As a food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USDA_wheat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3151" data-file-width="2150" decoding="async" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/USDA_wheat.jpg/220px-USDA_wheat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/USDA_wheat.jpg/330px-USDA_wheat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/USDA_wheat.jpg/440px-USDA_wheat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USDA_wheat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wheat is used in a wide variety of foods.</div></div></div>
