<p>Raw wheat can be ground into <a href="/wiki/Wheat_flour" title="Wheat flour">flour</a> or, using hard <a href="/wiki/Durum" title="Durum">durum wheat</a> only, can be ground into <a href="/wiki/Semolina" title="Semolina">semolina</a>; germinated and dried creating <a href="/wiki/Malt" title="Malt">malt</a>; crushed or cut into cracked wheat; parboiled (or steamed), dried, crushed and de-branned into <a href="/wiki/Bulgur" title="Bulgur">bulgur</a> also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Groats" title="Groats">groats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> If the raw wheat is broken into parts at the mill, as is usually done, the outer husk or <a href="/wiki/Bran" title="Bran">bran</a> can be used in several ways.
</p><p>Wheat is a major ingredient in such foods as <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">bread</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porridge" title="Porridge">porridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cracker_(food)" title="Cracker (food)">crackers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biscuit" title="Biscuit">biscuits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muesli" title="Muesli">muesli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancake" title="Pancake">pancakes</a>, pasta and <a href="/wiki/Noodle" title="Noodle">noodles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pie" title="Pie">pies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pastry" title="Pastry">pastries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pizza" title="Pizza">pizza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semolina" title="Semolina">semolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cake" title="Cake">cakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cookie" title="Cookie">cookies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muffin" title="Muffin">muffins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bread_roll" title="Bread roll">rolls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doughnut" title="Doughnut">doughnuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravy" title="Gravy">gravy</a>, beer, <a href="/wiki/Vodka" title="Vodka">vodka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boza" title="Boza">boza</a> (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermented_beverage" title="Fermented beverage">fermented beverage</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Breakfast_cereal" title="Breakfast cereal">breakfast cereals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>In manufacturing wheat products, <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a> is valuable to impart <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viscoelastic" title="Viscoelastic">viscoelastic</a> functional qualities in <a href="/wiki/Dough" title="Dough">dough</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry1_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-shewry1-101">[101]</a></sup> enabling the preparation of diverse processed foods such as breads, noodles, and pasta that facilitate wheat consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eufic_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-eufic-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry15_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-shewry15-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Nutrition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 100 grams, wheat provides 1,368 kilojoules (327 kilocalories) of <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a> and is a rich source (20% or more of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_Value" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a>, DV) of multiple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_nutrient" title="Essential nutrient">essential nutrients</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niacin_(nutrient)" title="Niacin (nutrient)">niacin</a> (table). Several <a href="/wiki/B_vitamins" title="B vitamins">B vitamins</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_minerals" title="Dietary minerals">dietary minerals</a> are in significant content. Wheat is 13% water, 71% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, and 1.5% fat. Its 13% protein content is mostly <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a> (75–80% of the protein in wheat).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry1_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-shewry1-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wheat proteins have a low quality for human nutrition, according to the new protein quality method (<a href="/wiki/Digestible_Indispensable_Amino_Acid_Score" title="Digestible Indispensable Amino Acid Score">DIAAS</a>) promoted by the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOproteinquality_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOproteinquality-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolfe2015_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolfe2015-103">[103]</a></sup> Though they contain adequate amounts of the other essential amino acids, at least for adults, wheat proteins are deficient in the <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">lysine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry15_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-shewry15-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO-EOLSS_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-EOLSS-104">[104]</a></sup> Because the proteins present in the wheat <a href="/wiki/Endosperm" title="Endosperm">endosperm</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a> proteins) are particularly poor in lysine, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_flour" title="White flour">white flours</a> are more deficient in lysine compared with whole grains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry15_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-shewry15-9">[9]</a></sup> Significant efforts in plant breeding are being made to develop lysine-rich wheat varieties, without success as of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOlysine_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOlysine-105">[105]</a></sup> Supplementation with proteins from other food sources (mainly <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a>) is commonly used to compensate for this deficiency,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOcerealsquality_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOcerealsquality-12">[12]</a></sup> since the limitation of a single essential amino acid causes the others to break down and become excreted, which is especially important during the period of growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry15_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-shewry15-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>

<td colspan="27">%DV = % daily value i.e. % of DRI (<a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Dietary Reference Intake</a>)
<p>Note: All nutrient values including protein and fiber are in %DV per 100 grams of the food item. Significant values are highlighted in light Gray color and bold letters. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
Cooking reduction = % Maximum typical reduction in nutrients due to boiling without draining for ovo-lacto-vegetables group<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NutritionalEffectsOfFoodProcessing_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-NutritionalEffectsOfFoodProcessing-109">[109]</a></sup>
Q = Quality of Protein in terms of completeness without adjusting for digestability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NutritionalEffectsOfFoodProcessing_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-NutritionalEffectsOfFoodProcessing-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>100 g (<span class="frac" role="math">3<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span> oz) of hard red winter wheat contain about 12.6 g of <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">protein</a>, 1.5 g of total fat, 71 g of <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a> (by difference), 12.2 g of <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>, and 3.2 mg of iron (17% of the daily requirement); the same weight of hard red spring wheat contains about 15.4 g of protein, 1.9 g of total fat, 68 g of carbohydrate (by difference), 12.2 g of dietary fiber, and 3.6 mg of iron (20% of the daily requirement).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDA_ARS_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDA_ARS-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldwide_production">Worldwide production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Worldwide production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wheat is grown on more than 218,000,000 hectares (540,000,000 acres).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOStat_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOStat-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common forms of wheat are white and red wheat. However, other natural forms of wheat exist. Other commercially minor but nutritionally promising species of naturally evolved wheat species include black, yellow and blue wheat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WheatBread_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-WheatBread-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_effects">Health effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Health effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Consumed worldwide by billions of people, wheat is a significant food for human nutrition, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Least_developed_countries" title="Least developed countries">least developed countries</a> where wheat products are primary foods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shewry_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shewry-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry15_9-7"><a href="#cite_note-shewry15-9">[9]</a></sup> When eaten as the <a href="/wiki/Whole_grain" title="Whole grain">whole grain</a>, wheat is a healthy food source of multiple nutrients and <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a> recommended for children and adults, in several daily servings containing a variety of foods that meet whole grain-rich criteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry15_9-8"><a href="#cite_note-shewry15-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eufic_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-eufic-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usda1_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-usda1-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usda2_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-usda2-115">[115]</a></sup> Dietary fiber may also help people feel full and therefore help with a healthy weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heart2016_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heart2016-116">[116]</a></sup> Further, wheat is a major source for natural and <a href="/wiki/Biofortification" title="Biofortification">biofortified</a> nutrient supplementation, including dietary fiber, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> and dietary <a href="/wiki/Mineral_(nutrient)" title="Mineral (nutrient)">minerals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hefferon_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-hefferon-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Manufacturers of foods containing wheat as a whole grain in specified amounts are allowed a <a href="/wiki/Health_claim" title="Health claim">health claim</a> for marketing purposes in the United States, stating: "low fat diets rich in fiber-containing grain products, fruits, and vegetables may reduce the risk of some types of cancer, a disease associated with many factors" and "diets low in saturated fat and cholesterol and rich in fruits, vegetables, and grain products that contain some types of dietary fiber, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soluble_fiber" title="Soluble fiber">soluble fiber</a>, may reduce the risk of heart disease, a disease associated with many factors".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The scientific opinion of the <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> (EFSA) related to health claims on gut health/bowel function, weight control, blood glucose/insulin levels, weight management, blood cholesterol, satiety, glycaemic index, digestive function and cardiovascular health is "that the food constituent, whole grain, (...) is not sufficiently characterised in relation to the claimed health effects" and "that a cause and effect relationship cannot be established between the consumption of whole grain and the claimed effects considered in this opinion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eufic_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-eufic-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EFSA2010_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-EFSA2010-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Concerns">Concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In genetically susceptible people, <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a> – a major part of wheat protein – can trigger <a href="/wiki/Coeliac_disease" title="Coeliac disease">coeliac disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shewry1_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-shewry1-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGOGuidelines2016_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-WGOGuidelines2016-121">[121]</a></sup> Coeliac disease affects about 1% of the general population in <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niddk_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-niddk-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGOGuidelines2016_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-WGOGuidelines2016-121">[121]</a></sup> There is evidence that most cases remain undiagnosed and untreated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGOGuidelines2016_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-WGOGuidelines2016-121">[121]</a></sup> The only known effective treatment is a strict lifelong <a href="/wiki/Gluten-free_diet" title="Gluten-free diet">gluten-free diet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGOGuidelines2016_121-3"><a href="#cite_note-WGOGuidelines2016-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>While coeliac disease is caused by a reaction to wheat proteins, it is not the same as a <a href="/wiki/Wheat_allergy" title="Wheat allergy">wheat allergy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niddk_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-niddk-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGOGuidelines2016_121-4"><a href="#cite_note-WGOGuidelines2016-121">[121]</a></sup> Other diseases <a href="/wiki/Gluten-related_disorders" title="Gluten-related disorders">triggered by eating wheat</a> are <a href="/wiki/Non-celiac_gluten_sensitivity" title="Non-celiac gluten sensitivity">non-coeliac gluten sensitivity</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niddk_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-niddk-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LudvigssonLeffler_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-LudvigssonLeffler-14">[14]</a></sup> (estimated to affect 0.5% to 13% of the general population<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MolinaInfanteSantolaria2015SystematicReview_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-MolinaInfanteSantolaria2015SystematicReview-123">[123]</a></sup>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gluten_ataxia" title="Gluten ataxia">gluten ataxia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis_herpetiformis" title="Dermatitis herpetiformis">dermatitis herpetiformis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LudvigssonLeffler_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-LudvigssonLeffler-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been speculated that <a href="/wiki/FODMAP" title="FODMAP">FODMAPs</a> present in wheat (mainly <a href="/wiki/Fructan" title="Fructan">fructans</a>) are the cause of non-coeliac gluten sensitivity. As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, reviews have concluded that FODMAPs only explain certain gastrointestinal symptoms, such as <a href="/wiki/Bloating" title="Bloating">bloating</a>, but not the <a href="/wiki/Non-celiac_gluten_sensitivity#Extraintestinal" title="Non-celiac gluten sensitivity">extra-digestive symptoms</a> that people with non-coeliac gluten sensitivity may develop, such as <a href="/wiki/Neurological_disorder" title="Neurological disorder">neurological disorders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fibromyalgia" title="Fibromyalgia">fibromyalgia</a>, psychological disturbances, and <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">dermatitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VoltaDeGiorgio2019_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-VoltaDeGiorgio2019-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verbeke2018_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verbeke2018-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FasanoSapone2015_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FasanoSapone2015-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other proteins present in wheat called amylase-trypsin inhibitors (ATIs) have been identified as the possible activator of the <a href="/wiki/Innate_immune_system" title="Innate immune system">innate immune system</a> in coeliac disease and non-coeliac gluten sensitivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FasanoSapone2015_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FasanoSapone2015-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verbeke2018_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Verbeke2018-125">[125]</a></sup> ATIs are part of the plant's natural defense against insects and may cause toll-like receptor 4 (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TLR4" title="TLR4">TLR4</a>)-mediated intestinal <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verbeke2018_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-Verbeke2018-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaroneTroncone2014_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-BaroneTroncone2014-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-junker-etal-2012_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-junker-etal-2012-128">[128]</a></sup> These TLR4-stimulating activities of ATIs are limited to gluten-containing cereals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FasanoSapone2015_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-FasanoSapone2015-126">[126]</a></sup> A 2017 study in mice demonstrated that ATIs exacerbate preexisting inflammation and might also worsen it at extraintestinal sites. This may explain why there is an increase of inflammation in people with preexisting diseases upon ingestion of ATIs-containing grains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verbeke2018_125-3"><a href="#cite_note-Verbeke2018-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_other_staple_foods">Comparison with other staple foods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Comparison with other staple foods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following table shows the nutrient content of wheat and other major staple foods in a raw form on a <a href="/wiki/Dry_matter#Dry_matter_basis" title="Dry matter">dry weight basis</a> to account for their different water contents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Raw forms of these staples, however, are not edible and cannot be digested. These must be sprouted, or prepared and cooked as appropriate for human consumption. In sprouted or cooked form, the relative nutritional and anti-nutritional contents of each of these staples is remarkably different from that of the raw form, as reported in this table.
</p><p>In cooked form, the nutrition value for each staple depends on the cooking method (for example: baking, boiling, steaming, frying, etc.).

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_use">Commercial use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Commercial use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WheatYield.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/WheatYield.png/450px-WheatYield.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/WheatYield.png/675px-WheatYield.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/WheatYield.png/900px-WheatYield.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WheatYield.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of worldwide wheat production.</div></div></div>
<p>Harvested wheat grain that enters trade is classified according to grain properties for the purposes of the <a href="/wiki/Commodity_market" title="Commodity market">commodity-</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a> markets. Wheat buyers use these to decide which wheat to buy, as each class has special uses, and producers use them to decide which classes of wheat will be most profitable to cultivate.
</p><p>Wheat is widely cultivated as a <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crop</a> because it produces a good yield per unit area, grows well in a <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">temperate climate</a> even with a moderately short <a href="/wiki/Growing_season" title="Growing season">growing season</a>, and yields a versatile, high-quality flour that is widely used in <a href="/wiki/Baking" title="Baking">baking</a>. Most breads are made with wheat flour, including many breads named for the other grains they contain, for example, most <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oat" title="Oat">oat</a> breads. The popularity of foods made from wheat flour creates a large demand for the grain, even in economies with significant food <a href="/wiki/Economic_surplus" title="Economic surplus">surpluses</a>.
</p><p>In recent years, low international wheat prices have often encouraged farmers in the United States to change to more profitable crops. In 1998, the price at harvest of a 60 pounds (27 kg) <a href="/wiki/Bushel" title="Bushel">bushel</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G4020_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-G4020-131">[131]</a></sup> was $2.68 per.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ali_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-ali-132">[132]</a></sup> Some information providers, following <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Board_of_Trade" title="Chicago Board of Trade">CBOT</a> practice, quote the wheat market in per <a href="/wiki/Ton" title="Ton">ton</a> denomination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> A USDA report revealed that in 1998, average operating costs were $1.43 per bushel and total costs were $3.97 per bushel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ali_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-ali-132">[132]</a></sup> In that study, farm wheat yields averaged 41.7 bushels per acre (2.2435 metric ton/hectare), and typical total wheat production value was $31,900 per farm, with total farm production value (including other crops) of $173,681 per farm, plus $17,402 in government payments. There were significant profitability differences between low- and high-cost farms, due to crop yield differences, location, and farm size.
</p>

<tr>
<td colspan="6" style="text-align: center;"><small>Source: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FAO" title="FAO">UN Food and Agriculture Organization</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-fao-136">[136]</a></sup></small>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Production_of_wheat_(2019).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="652" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Production_of_wheat_%282019%29.svg/300px-Production_of_wheat_%282019%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Production_of_wheat_%282019%29.svg/450px-Production_of_wheat_%282019%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Production_of_wheat_%282019%29.svg/600px-Production_of_wheat_%282019%29.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Production_of_wheat_(2019).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Production of wheat (2019)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_prices_in_England,_OWID.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Wheat_prices_in_England%2C_OWID.svg/350px-Wheat_prices_in_England%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Wheat_prices_in_England%2C_OWID.svg/525px-Wheat_prices_in_England%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Wheat_prices_in_England%2C_OWID.svg/700px-Wheat_prices_in_England%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_prices_in_England,_OWID.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wheat prices in England, 1264–1996<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 2020, world wheat production was 761 million tonnes, led by China, India, and Russia collectively providing 38% of the world total.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-fao-136">[136]</a></sup> As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_wheat_exports" title="List of countries by wheat exports">the largest exporters were</a> Russia (32 million tonnes), United States (27), Canada (23) and France (20), while the largest importers were Indonesia (11 million tonnes), Egypt (10.4) and Turkey (10.0).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_factors">Historical factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Historical factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Empire_and_successor_states">British Empire and successor states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: British Empire and successor states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Wheat became a central agriculture endeavor in the worldwide <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> in the 19th century, and remains of great importance in Australia, Canada and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> In Australia, with vast lands and a limited work force, expanded production depended on technological advances, especially regarding irrigation and machinery. By the 1840s there were 900 growers in <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a>. They used the "Ridley's Stripper", to remove the heads of grain, and the reaper-harvester perfected by <a href="/wiki/John_Ridley_(inventor)" title="John Ridley (inventor)">John Ridley</a> in 1843.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>  By 1850 South Australia had become the granary for the region; soon wheat farming spread to Victoria and New South Wales, with heavy exports to Great Britain. In Canada modern farm implements made large scale wheat farming possible from the late 1840s on. By the 1879s Saskatchewan was the center, followed by Alberta, Manitoba and Ontario, as the spread of railway lines allowed easy exports to Britain. By 1910 wheat made up 22% of Canada's exports, rising to 25% in 1930 despite the sharp decline in prices during the worldwide <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Efforts to expand wheat production in South Africa, Kenya and India were stymied by low yields and disease. However by 2000 India had become the second largest producer of wheat in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the 19th century the American wheat frontier moved rapidly westward.  By the 1880s 70% of American exports went to British ports.  The first successful <a href="/wiki/Grain_elevator" title="Grain elevator">grain elevator</a> was built  in Buffalo in 1842.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>  The cost of transport fell rapidly.  In 1869 it cost 37 cents to transport a bushel of wheat from Chicago to Liverpool. In 1905 it was 10 cents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the 20th century, global wheat output expanded by about 5-fold, but until about 1955 most of this reflected increases in wheat crop area, with lesser (about 20%) increases in crop yields per unit area. After 1955 however, there was a ten-fold increase in the rate of wheat yield improvement per year, and this became the major factor allowing global wheat production to increase. Thus technological innovation and scientific crop management with <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">synthetic nitrogen fertilizer</a>, irrigation and wheat breeding were the main drivers of wheat output growth in the second half of the century. There were some significant decreases in wheat crop area, for instance in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Better seed storage and germination ability (and hence a smaller requirement to retain harvested crop for next year's seed) is another 20th-century technological innovation. In Medieval England, farmers saved one-quarter of their wheat harvest as seed for the next crop, leaving only three-quarters for food and feed consumption. By 1999, the global average seed use of wheat was about 6% of output.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Several factors are currently slowing the rate of global expansion of wheat production: population growth rates are falling while wheat yields continue to rise. There is evidence, however, that rising temperatures associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">climate change</a> are reducing wheat yield in several locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> In addition, the better economic profitability of other crops such as soybeans and maize, linked with investment in modern genetic technologies, has promoted shifts to other crops.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Farming_systems">Farming systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Farming systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2014, the most productive <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop yields</a> for wheat were in Ireland, producing 10 tonnes per hectare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao-prod2014_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-fao-prod2014-7">[7]</a></sup> In addition to gaps in farming system technology and knowledge, some large wheat grain-producing countries have significant losses after harvest at the farm and because of poor roads, inadequate storage technologies, inefficient supply chains and farmers' inability to bring the produce into retail markets dominated by small shopkeepers. Various studies in India, for example, have concluded that about 10% of total wheat production is lost at farm level, another 10% is lost because of poor storage and road networks, and additional amounts lost at the retail level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab region</a> of the Indian subcontinent, as well as North China, irrigation has been a major contributor to increased grain output. More widely over the last 40 years, a massive increase in fertilizer use together with the increased availability of semi-dwarf varieties in developing countries, has greatly increased yields per hectare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-godfray_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-godfray-8">[8]</a></sup> In developing countries, use of (mainly nitrogenous) fertilizer increased 25-fold in this period. However, farming systems rely on much more than fertilizer and breeding to improve productivity. A good illustration of this is Australian wheat growing in the southern winter cropping zone, where, despite low rainfall (300 mm), wheat cropping is successful even with relatively little use of nitrogenous fertilizer. This is achieved by 'rotation cropping' (traditionally called the ley system) with leguminous pastures and, in the last decade, including a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canola" title="Canola">canola</a> crop in the rotations has boosted wheat yields by a further 25%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> In these low rainfall areas, better use of available soil-water (and better control of soil erosion) is achieved by retaining the stubble after harvesting and by minimizing tillage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_variation">Geographical variation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Geographical variation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are substantial differences in wheat farming, trading, policy, sector growth, and wheat uses in different regions of the world. The <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_wheat_exports" title="List of countries by wheat exports">largest exporters</a> of wheat in 2016 were, in order of exported quantities: Russian Federation (25.3 million tonnes), United States (24.0 million tonnes), Canada (19.7 million tonnes), France (18.3 million tonnes), and Australia (16.1 million tonnes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao-exp2016_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-fao-exp2016-151">[151]</a></sup> The largest importers of wheat in 2016 were, in order of imported quantities: Indonesia (10.5 million tonnes), Egypt (8.7 million tonnes), Algeria (8.2 million tonnes), Italy (7.7 million tonnes) and Spain (7.0 million tonnes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao-exp2016_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-fao-exp2016-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the rapidly developing countries of Asia and Africa, westernization of diets associated with increasing prosperity is leading to growth in <i>per capita</i> demand for wheat at the expense of the other food staples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-godfray_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-godfray-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Most_productive">Most productive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Most productive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The average annual world farm yield for wheat in 2014 was 3.3 tonnes per hectare (330 grams per square meter).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao-prod2014_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-fao-prod2014-7">[7]</a></sup> Ireland's wheat farms were the most productive in 2014, with a nationwide average of 10.0 tonnes per hectare, followed by the Netherlands (9.2), and Germany, New Zealand and the United Kingdom (each with 8.6).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao-prod2014_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-fao-prod2014-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Futures_contracts">Futures contracts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Futures contracts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wheat <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures</a> are traded on the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Board_of_Trade" title="Chicago Board of Trade">Chicago Board of Trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Board_of_Trade" title="Kansas City Board of Trade">Kansas City Board of Trade</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis_Grain_Exchange" title="Minneapolis Grain Exchange">Minneapolis Grain Exchange</a>, and have delivery dates in March (H), May (K), July (N), September (U), and December (Z).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peak_wheat">Peak wheat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Peak wheat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Peak_wheat" title="Peak wheat">Peak wheat</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Peak_wheat&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Food_production_per_capita.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/220px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/330px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/440px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Food_production_per_capita.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Food production per person increased since 1961.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peak_wheat" title="Peak wheat">Peak wheat</a> is the concept that <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural</a> production, due to its high use of water and energy inputs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> is subject to the <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">same profile as oil</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>  The central tenet is that a point is reached, the "peak", beyond which agricultural production plateaus and does not grow any further,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>  and may even go into permanent decline.
</p><p>Based on current <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply and demand</a> factors for agricultural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commodities" title="Commodities">commodities</a> (e.g., changing diets in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emerging_economies" title="Emerging economies">emerging economies</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels" title="Biofuels">biofuels</a>, declining acreage under irrigation, growing <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">global population</a>, stagnant <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_productivity" title="Agricultural productivity">agricultural productivity</a> growth<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>), some commentators are predicting a long-term annual production shortfall of around 2% which, based on the highly inelastic <a href="/wiki/Demand_curve" title="Demand curve">demand curve</a> for food crops, could lead to sustained price increases in excess of 10% a year - sufficient to double crop prices in seven years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Resources_Institute" title="World Resources Institute">World Resources Institute</a>, global per capita food production has been increasing substantially for the past several decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Agronomy">Agronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Agronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spiklet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="623" data-file-width="525" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Spiklet.jpg/170px-Spiklet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Spiklet.jpg/255px-Spiklet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Spiklet.jpg/340px-Spiklet.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spiklet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wheat spikelet with the three anthers sticking out</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crop_development">Crop development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Crop development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wheat normally needs between 110 and 130 days between sowing and harvest, depending upon climate, seed type, and soil conditions (<a href="/wiki/Winter_wheat" title="Winter wheat">winter wheat</a> lies dormant during a winter freeze). Optimal crop management requires that the farmer have a detailed understanding of each stage of development in the growing plants. In particular, spring <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plant_hormone" title="Plant hormone">growth regulators</a> are typically applied only at specific stages of plant development. For example, it is currently recommended that the second application of nitrogen is best done when the ear (not visible at this stage) is about 1 cm in size (Z31 on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zadoks_scale" title="Zadoks scale">Zadoks scale</a>). Knowledge of stages is also important to identify periods of higher risk from the climate. For example, pollen formation from the mother cell, and the stages between <a href="/wiki/Anthesis" title="Anthesis">anthesis</a> and maturity, are susceptible to high temperatures, and this adverse effect is made worse by water stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Farmers also benefit from knowing when the 'flag leaf' (last leaf) appears, as this leaf represents about 75% of photosynthesis reactions during the grain filling period, and so should be preserved from disease or insect attacks to ensure a good yield.
</p><p>Several systems exist to identify crop stages, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feekes_scale" title="Feekes scale">Feekes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zadoks_scale" title="Zadoks scale">Zadoks scales</a> being the most widely used. Each scale is a standard system which describes successive stages reached by the crop during the agricultural season.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" <div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests_and_diseases">Pests and diseases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Pests and diseases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Pests<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmerSocAgr-pest-def_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmerSocAgr-pest-def-163">[163]</a></sup> – or pests and diseases, depending on the definition – consume 21.47% of the world's wheat crop annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Savary-et-al-2019_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Savary-et-al-2019-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Diseases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wheat_diseases" title="Wheat diseases">Wheat diseases</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_wheat_diseases" title="List of wheat diseases">List of wheat diseases</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_10772_Rustaffected_wheat_seedlings.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2657" data-file-width="1765" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/CSIRO_ScienceImage_10772_Rustaffected_wheat_seedlings.jpg/170px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_10772_Rustaffected_wheat_seedlings.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/CSIRO_ScienceImage_10772_Rustaffected_wheat_seedlings.jpg/255px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_10772_Rustaffected_wheat_seedlings.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/CSIRO_ScienceImage_10772_Rustaffected_wheat_seedlings.jpg/340px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_10772_Rustaffected_wheat_seedlings.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_10772_Rustaffected_wheat_seedlings.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rust-affected wheat seedlings</div></div></div>
<p>There are many wheat diseases, mainly caused by fungi, bacteria, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viruses" title="Viruses">viruses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transgenic_plant" title="Transgenic plant">Plant breeding</a> to develop new disease-resistant varieties, and sound crop management practices are important for preventing disease. Fungicides, used to prevent the significant crop losses from fungal disease, can be a significant variable cost in wheat production. Estimates of the amount of wheat production lost owing to plant diseases vary between 10 and 25% in Missouri.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> A wide range of organisms infect wheat, of which the most important are viruses and fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main wheat-disease categories are:
</p>
<ul><li>Seed-borne diseases: these include seed-borne scab, seed-borne <i><a href="/wiki/Stagonospora" title="Stagonospora">Stagonospora</a></i> (previously known as <i>Septoria</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_bunt" title="Common bunt">common bunt</a> (stinking smut), and <a href="/wiki/Loose_smut" title="Loose smut">loose smut</a>. These are managed with <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicides</a>.</li>
<li>Leaf- and head- <a href="/wiki/Blight" title="Blight">blight</a> diseases: Powdery mildew, <a href="/wiki/Wheat_leaf_rust" title="Wheat leaf rust">leaf rust</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Septoria_tritici" title="Septoria tritici">Septoria tritici</a></i> leaf blotch, <i>Stagonospora</i> (<i>Septoria</i>) nodorum leaf and glume blotch, and <i><a href="/wiki/Fusarium" title="Fusarium">Fusarium</a></i> head scab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></li>
<li>Crown and <a href="/wiki/Root_rot" title="Root rot">root rot</a> diseases: Two of the more important of these are '<a href="/wiki/Take-all" title="Take-all">take-all</a>' and <i><a href="/wiki/Cephalosporium_gramineum" title="Cephalosporium gramineum">Cephalosporium</a></i> stripe. Both of these diseases are soil borne.</li>
<li>Stem rust diseases: Caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basidiomycete" title="Basidiomycete">basidiomycete</a> fungi e.g. <a href="/wiki/Ug99" title="Ug99">Ug99</a></li>
<li>Viral diseases: <a href="/wiki/Wheat_spindle_streak_mosaic_virus" title="Wheat spindle streak mosaic virus">Wheat spindle streak mosaic</a> (yellow mosaic) and <a href="/wiki/Barley_yellow_dwarf" title="Barley yellow dwarf">barley yellow dwarf</a> are the two most common viral diseases. Control can be achieved by using resistant varieties.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_pests">Animal pests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wheat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Animal pests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wheat is used as a food plant by the <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larvae</a> of some <a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a> (<a href="/wiki/Butterfly" title="Butterfly">butterfly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moth</a>) species including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flame_(moth)" title="Flame (moth)">the flame</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rustic_shoulder-knot" title="Rustic shoulder-knot">rustic shoulder-knot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Setaceous_Hebrew_character" title="Setaceous Hebrew character">setaceous Hebrew character</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turnip_moth" title="Turnip moth">turnip moth</a>.
Early in the season, many species of birds, including the <a href="/wiki/Long-tailed_widowbird" title="Long-tailed widowbird">long-tailed widowbird</a>, and rodents feed upon wheat crops. These animals can cause significant damage to a crop by digging up and eating newly planted seeds or young plants. They can also damage the crop late in the season by eating the grain from the mature spike. Recent post-harvest losses in cereals amount to billions of dollars per year in the United States alone, and damage to wheat by various borers, beetles and weevils is no exception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Rodents can also cause major losses during storage, and in major grain growing regions, field mice numbers can sometimes build up explosively to plague proportions because of the ready availability of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> To reduce the amount of wheat lost to post-harvest pests, <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Research_Service" title="Agricultural Research Service">Agricultural Research Service</a> scientists have developed an "insect-o-graph", which can detect insects in wheat that are not visible to the naked eye. The device uses electrical signals to detect the insects as the wheat is being milled. The new technology is so precise that it can detect 5–10 infested seeds out of 30,000 good ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Tracking insect infestations in stored grain is critical for food safety as well as for the marketing value of the crop.
</p>
